<div id="descriptionContainer">
      <div style="right:0" class="readable stacked" id="description">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer16762111683194049686">In a Carmelite monastery outside present-day Los Angeles, life goes on in a manner virtually un-changed for centuries. Sister John of the Cross has spent years there in the service of God. And there, she alone experiences visions of such dazzling power and insight that she is looked upon as a spiritual master. <br /><br />But Sister John's visions are accompanied by powerful headaches</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText16762111683194049686">In a Carmelite monastery outside present-day Los Angeles, life goes on in a manner virtually un-changed for centuries. Sister John of the Cross has spent years there in the service of God. And there, she alone experiences visions of such dazzling power and insight that she is looked upon as a spiritual master. <br /><br />But Sister John's visions are accompanied by powerful headaches, and when a doctor reveals that they may be dangerous, she faces a devastating choice. For if her spiritual gifts are symptoms of illness rather than grace, will a "cure" mean the end of her visions and a soul once again dry and searching?<br /><br />This is the dilemma at the heart of Mark Salzman's spare, astonishing new novel. With extraordinary dexterity, the author of the best-selling <strong>Iron &amp; Silk</strong> and <strong>The Soloist</strong> brings to life the mysterious world of the cloister, giving us a brilliantly realized portrait of women today drawn to the rigors of an ancient religious life, and of one woman's trial at the perilous intersection of faith and reason. <br /><br /><strong>Lying Awake</strong> is a novel of remarkable empathy and imagination, and Mark Salzman's most provocative work to date.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="16762111683194049686">...more</a>

        $$('span.elementTwo').each(function (e) {
          if (e.getStyle('display') !== 'none') {
            var lessLink = e.next();
            swapContent(lessLink);
          }
        });
      });

//]]&gt;
</script>  <script type="text/javascript">
  //&lt;![CDATA[
      var $ratingGraph = $j('#rating_graph');
      var $copy;
      if ($ratingGraph.length &gt; 0) {
          $copy = $ratingGraph.clone();
          $j('#rating_details_tip_graph').remove();
          $copy.attr('id', 'rating_details_tip_graph');
          $copy.find('script').remove();
          $j('#rating_details_tip').prepend($copy);
      }
  //]]&gt;
  </script>
</div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_204883385">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/204883385" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/1309716-sarah" class="left imgcol" title="Sarah"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1450743936p2/1309716.jpg" alt="Sarah" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/204883385?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Sep 06, 2011</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1309716-sarah" class="user" title="Sarah">Sarah</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/136228.Lying_Awake" title="Paperback 9780747561408" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer204883385">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5014187923554078729">Sister John is a Carmelite nun who, after years of dedicated service, begins to experience visions of the divine in ecstatic, crystalline clarity. But these visions are accompanied by terrible headaches, and Sister John is encouraged to seek medical attention. When told by a neurologist that her experiences are likely the result of (curable) epileptic seizures, Sister John is forced to rethink everything she knows about spiritual life.<br /><br />On the surface, this seems like a(nother) religion vs. scienc</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText5014187923554078729">Sister John is a Carmelite nun who, after years of dedicated service, begins to experience visions of the divine in ecstatic, crystalline clarity. But these visions are accompanied by terrible headaches, and Sister John is encouraged to seek medical attention. When told by a neurologist that her experiences are likely the result of (curable) epileptic seizures, Sister John is forced to rethink everything she knows about spiritual life.<br /><br />On the surface, this seems like a(nother) religion vs. science narrative. But, this isn't simply a matter of conflicting paradigms. That's the easy part. It's the human element, the choices we have to make on our own that are difficult.<br /><br />Sister John is genuinely good and genuinely selfless. She's so selfless and good, I very nearly abandoned the book! I couldn't relate to her. I felt Mark Salzman was keeping too much of a respectful distance. But, as the story develops, we begin to see Sister John's frailty, her human fallibility. And, yes, her <em>self</em>. When the most meaningful experiences of her life are reduced to a neurological defect, your heart breaks for her. Salzman sees the beauty in her vocation...but he doesn't romanticize it.<br /><br />He's not trying to make Catholicism hip, <em>Evelyn Waugh</em>. He's not trying to make it sexy, <em>Graham Greene</em>. He just wrote a beautiful, poignantly human narrative. And, you don't have to be Catholic to appreciate it.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="5014187923554078729">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/21851053?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">May 13, 2008</a>

      <a href="/user/show/849321-jeana" class="user" title="Jeana">Jeana</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/136228.Lying_Awake" title="Paperback 9780747561408" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/849321-jeana?shelf=good-fiction" class="actionLinkLite">good-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a onclick="Effect.toggle('reviewTextContainer21851053'); return false;" href="#">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span style="display:none" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer21851053">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13390055893054536541">I ended up liking this book a whole lot more than I thought I was while I was reading it. In fact, when I wasn't reading it, I would think about something in it that seemed to resonate with me. For example, I thought it interesting to think that "God loves each of us as if there were no one else on earth. Believing that is the most perfect act of faith of all."<br /><br />The book is about a nun who finds out after years of feeling like she was directly experiencing God's presence that she had epilepsy and</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText13390055893054536541">I ended up liking this book a whole lot more than I thought I was while I was reading it. In fact, when I wasn't reading it, I would think about something in it that seemed to resonate with me. For example, I thought it interesting to think that "God loves each of us as if there were no one else on earth. Believing that is the most perfect act of faith of all."<br /><br />The book is about a nun who finds out after years of feeling like she was directly experiencing God's presence that she had epilepsy and that most of what she felt was due to her seizures. She has a surgery to eliminate the problem and wonders whether she should return. After all, wasn't it just the epilepsy that caused her to feel so intimate with God? <br /><br />I particularly loved the ending. Because all the nuns admitted to doubting their decisions to devote their lives to God. But they were still there, trying. And that was the point to it all. And isn't that the point to what any of us who believes in God do? It takes so much more to keep saying "yes" to doing things when we don't have a complete faith. As Mother Emmanuel said to Sister John, "Yet you're still here, trying to do His will anyway. That's the kind of understanding I meant. The doing kind, not the knowing kind." <br /><br />One other quote from the book I liked was this: ". . . no matter how many times we hear what it costs to follow Christ, we're still shocked when the bill comes, and we wonder all over again if we can pay it. If we make an act of faith then, it counts more than on the days when we feel sure." <br /><br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="13390055893054536541">...more</a>

          </span>

      <a href="/user/show/2582387-marvin" class="user" title="Marvin">Marvin</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/136228.Lying_Awake" title="Paperback 9780747561408" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/2582387-marvin?shelf=religion" class="actionLinkLite">religion</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer67118170">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14697410319717063551">A wise, gentle book with a very different feel from Salzman's earlier book, The Soloist. It is unashamedly filled with religious language, rendered faithfully and sympathetically. We really feel like we get inside the religious struggle of a longtime nun--and do so, miraculously, without psychologizing. This nun, who struggles for 25 years to know God, finally has a spiritual breakthrough just as she develops severe headaches that turn out to be caused by mild epileptic seizures. Does this mean</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText14697410319717063551">A wise, gentle book with a very different feel from Salzman's earlier book, The Soloist. It is unashamedly filled with religious language, rendered faithfully and sympathetically. We really feel like we get inside the religious struggle of a longtime nun--and do so, miraculously, without psychologizing. This nun, who struggles for 25 years to know God, finally has a spiritual breakthrough just as she develops severe headaches that turn out to be caused by mild epileptic seizures. Does this mean that her religious experience, which she has written about in a well-received book, was inauthentic? What does that mean for her personal, intimate relationship with God, her calling, and her role in the community? These are hard questions which Salzman explores remarkably faithfully &amp; deeply without offering easy or pat answers. This nun's humility &amp; selfless seeking offer a striking contrast to the (however sympathetically rendered) exclusive, arrogant, selfish striving of the main Pentecostal character in The Hallelujah Side. Even more amazing, this spiritually deep &amp; rich book is a very accessible read that can be read in a couple of hours. I was, however, puzzled by chapter titles that seem to bear no relation to the chapter's content. I must have missed something.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="14697410319717063551">...more</a>

          </span>

      <a href="/user/show/1328950-judy" class="user" title="Judy">Judy</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

              <a href="/review/list/1328950-judy?shelf=my-2012-books" class="actionLinkLite">my-2012-books</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer357243320">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17336325195193948181">[Mark Salzman's Lying Awake is a finely wrought gem that plumbs the depths of one woman's soul, and in so doing raises salient questions about the power-and price-of faith.] - Goodreads<br /><br />This book languished on my bookshelf for several years before I finally got around to reading it. It's a thought-provoking story about a nun in a very small cloister of Carmelite nuns in Los Angeles. Nearly all of the story takes place within these walls. Sister John has been here as a nun for many years, and yet</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText17336325195193948181">[Mark Salzman's Lying Awake is a finely wrought gem that plumbs the depths of one woman's soul, and in so doing raises salient questions about the power-and price-of faith.] - Goodreads<br /><br />This book languished on my bookshelf for several years before I finally got around to reading it. It's a thought-provoking story about a nun in a very small cloister of Carmelite nuns in Los Angeles. Nearly all of the story takes place within these walls. Sister John has been here as a nun for many years, and yet for a long time she feels that she still has not truly met god. She begins to experience severe headaches, but the headaches are accompanied by a transcendent experience that she feels bring her close to god. What happens beyond this point is interesting and seems akin to some of the more mundane decisions and turning points we all come to in our lives at some point. As much as I disdane organized religion, I find stories about it somehow fascinating. The story was gently and skillfully told, I recommend it. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="17336325195193948181">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_26579834">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/26579834" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/237249-keleigh" class="left imgcol" title="Keleigh"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1221249744p2/237249.jpg" alt="Keleigh" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/26579834?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jul 10, 2008</a>

      <a href="/user/show/237249-keleigh" class="user" title="Keleigh">Keleigh</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/136228.Lying_Awake" title="Paperback 9780747561408" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/237249-keleigh?shelf=floatingether" class="actionLinkLite">floatingether</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/237249-keleigh?shelf=yearnings" class="actionLinkLite">yearnings</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer26579834">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11523981217331947390">Salzman's writing is quiet and precise, as unobtrusive as a nun's rustling skirts. It went a little slow at first, but gradually I grew accustomed to the slow and meditative pace, and became entirely engaged in the cloistered life of his characters. Sister John of the Cross faces a stark predicament involving a health condition, a form of epilepsy that produces rapturous mental states--a phenomenon shared by Dostoevsky, who described it in 3rd person in <em>The Idiot</em>:<br /><br />"He remembered that he always ha</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText11523981217331947390">Salzman's writing is quiet and precise, as unobtrusive as a nun's rustling skirts. It went a little slow at first, but gradually I grew accustomed to the slow and meditative pace, and became entirely engaged in the cloistered life of his characters. Sister John of the Cross faces a stark predicament involving a health condition, a form of epilepsy that produces rapturous mental states--a phenomenon shared by Dostoevsky, who described it in 3rd person in <em>The Idiot</em>:<br /><br />"He remembered that he always had one minute just before the epleptic fit when suddenly in the midst of sadness, spiritual darkness and oppression, there seemed at moments a flash of light in his brain, and with extraordinary impetus all his vital forces suddenly began working at their highest tension. The sense of life, the consciousness of self, were multiplied ten times at these moments which passed like a flash of lightning. His mind and heart were flooded with extraordinary light... But these moments, these flashes, were only the prelude of that final second in which the fit began."<br /><br />FD went on to say that regardless of the "abnormalcy" of his disease that those moments were of "the highest form of existence" and "the acme of harmony and beauty". He concludes with a Rumiesque phrase:<br /><br />"at the very last conscious moment before the fit began, he had time to say to himself clearly and consciously, 'Yes, for this moment one might give one's whole life!'"<br /><br />Salzman's realistic account of Carmelite mysticism was sensitive and sympathetic, but also maintained a sense of objectivity. Sister John's decision is not overly-dramatized, nor are her rapturous fits described with obvious overtones of "specialness." Reading this, I understood better what meditation teacher Shinzen Young describes in "The Science of Enlightenment": the underlying commonality of all mystical traditions being simple mindfulness and equanimity. Closeness to God through renunciation of self, in the least romantic sense of the term.<br /><br />Check out an interview with the author on Salon.com after you read the book (truly a quick read), located <a rel="nofollow" href="http://archive.salon.com/people/conv/2001/01/10/salzman/">here</a>.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="11523981217331947390">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <a href="/user/show/901783-happyreader" class="user" title="Happyreader">Happyreader</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer16825367">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13963726054554421447">A humane portrayal of the struggle to connect to something beyond oneself. A central question posed by this book is what makes a spiritual life worthwhile. Are positive religous states beneficial or addictive? As the priest says in response to Sister John's fears about losing her mystical experiences: "The problem is, you're still looking out for number one." Contrast that to the doctor's confession about almost quitting medicine during his first year of residency because he realized he had gott</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText13963726054554421447">A humane portrayal of the struggle to connect to something beyond oneself. A central question posed by this book is what makes a spiritual life worthwhile. Are positive religous states beneficial or addictive? As the priest says in response to Sister John's fears about losing her mystical experiences: "The problem is, you're still looking out for number one." Contrast that to the doctor's confession about almost quitting medicine during his first year of residency because he realized he had gotten into medicine for the wrong reasons but remained when he found out "that <em>everybody</em> gets into medicine for the wrong reasons. It comes with the territory." At the end of the day, it's the commitment, community, and daily practice that sustains Sister John, despite the struggle.</span>

              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer772849633">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8463440175082020252">Lying Awake is a Carmelite Nun's journey through ecstatic conversation with God and a decision about her health that is likely to remove that closeness from her life. The writing itself is sparse and internal, very much like the inner life I imagine a nun to have. Beautiful and kept me up reading late into the night.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer839325213">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4480443393433664144">A quiet, reflective book. Sister John of the Cross, a Carmelite nun in a small, closed order in Los Angeles is an inspiration to her fellow nuns for the depth of her devotion. Her debilitating headaches become worse, and in seeking treatent, it becomes clear that the epilepsy which causes her pain may also be the source of her visions. How should she then proceed? Should she seek treatment or not? Salzman treats the women of the convent with delicacy and respect. Nothing could be more alien to m</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText4480443393433664144">A quiet, reflective book. Sister John of the Cross, a Carmelite nun in a small, closed order in Los Angeles is an inspiration to her fellow nuns for the depth of her devotion. Her debilitating headaches become worse, and in seeking treatent, it becomes clear that the epilepsy which causes her pain may also be the source of her visions. How should she then proceed? Should she seek treatment or not? Salzman treats the women of the convent with delicacy and respect. Nothing could be more alien to me than the thought of joining a closed order, yet I read this story with interest, sympathy and a degree of understanding. It's written in a touching, economical style that seems to reflect the stark simplicity of the life of the sisters. It's sympathetc without romanticising. A fine book</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="4480443393433664144">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_1436672008">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1436672008" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/10876643-jeff" class="left imgcol" title="Jeff"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1440785469p2/10876643.jpg" alt="Jeff" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1436672008?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Dec 19, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/10876643-jeff" class="user" title="Jeff">Jeff</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/136228.Lying_Awake" title="Paperback 9780747561408" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/10876643-jeff?shelf=spiritually-significant-fiction" class="actionLinkLite">spiritually-significant-fiction</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/10876643-jeff?shelf=catholic-literature" class="actionLinkLite">catholic-literature</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/10876643-jeff?shelf=christian-fiction" class="actionLinkLite">christian-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1436672008">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17662039326199422136">"Didn't [St.] Teresa also warn that the price of following a dream includes painful setbacks, even having to start all over again? Sometimes it means facing things that we think we can't face, to learn the depth of God's mystery and of our need for faith.<br /><br />My God, I feel as if I am being torn apart."<br /><br />Sister John of the Cross is going through her own "dark night of the soul." She sees the world through a kaleidoscopic lens, seeing spirituality in everything. However, she soon learns that her spiritu</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText17662039326199422136">"Didn't [St.] Teresa also warn that the price of following a dream includes painful setbacks, even having to start all over again? Sometimes it means facing things that we think we can't face, to learn the depth of God's mystery and of our need for faith.<br /><br />My God, I feel as if I am being torn apart."<br /><br />Sister John of the Cross is going through her own "dark night of the soul." She sees the world through a kaleidoscopic lens, seeing spirituality in everything. However, she soon learns that her spiritual ecstasy is the result of epilepsy. With an operation, she can be healed, but she may also lose what is most meaningful to her.<br /><br />This is not a false dichotomy of science vs faith story. It is the story of a woman struggling with faith, doubt, suffering, and God. It is beautifully written, poetic, and well-researched.<br /><br />"We all have to try to become holy on our own, and fail, before we can approach God with humility."</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="17662039326199422136">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer8985297270734000099">The appeal of the monastic life is evident to those attracted to martial arts and romantic versions of Buddhism. Salzman, whose previous books entailed his concern with things Eastern and Wise, has written a fantastic account of life in a Californian Carmelite convent. Salzman worked in a youth prison while he was struggling with writing this novel. In contrast to the chatter of teen felons in <em>True Notebooks,</em> this novel walks softly through the austere settings and stark schedules of the nuns; y</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText8985297270734000099">The appeal of the monastic life is evident to those attracted to martial arts and romantic versions of Buddhism. Salzman, whose previous books entailed his concern with things Eastern and Wise, has written a fantastic account of life in a Californian Carmelite convent. Salzman worked in a youth prison while he was struggling with writing this novel. In contrast to the chatter of teen felons in <em>True Notebooks,</em> this novel walks softly through the austere settings and stark schedules of the nuns; you can hear a pin drop or the emotional dynamics in a room change. The plot is about a nun whose mystical experiences, and successful spiritual writings, may or may not be the result of epilepsy. A sojourn to a hospital reveals that medicine too requires discipline, obedience and attention. Besides the question of supernatural sources of spiritual vision, this fine novel is more rewarding about doubt, faith and struggle: “God’s presence was replaced by an atmosphere of human compromise.” Amen!</span>

              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer123290607">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8857799971240963677">"Sister John of the Cross pushed her blanket aside, dropped to her knees on the floor of the cell, and offered the day to God."<br /><br />I'm not sure why, being raised Protestant, I wanted to read this slim novel, but I did. Perhaps I was curious about the daily life of a cloistered Catholic nun, or perhaps it was simply that I enjoyed Salzman's other books. I enjoyed this one too. Sister John is an interesting person, not at all certain of either her faith or her vocational choice. After years in the con</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText8857799971240963677">"Sister John of the Cross pushed her blanket aside, dropped to her knees on the floor of the cell, and offered the day to God."<br /><br />I'm not sure why, being raised Protestant, I wanted to read this slim novel, but I did. Perhaps I was curious about the daily life of a cloistered Catholic nun, or perhaps it was simply that I enjoyed Salzman's other books. I enjoyed this one too. Sister John is an interesting person, not at all certain of either her faith or her vocational choice. After years in the convent, struggling with her faith, she has a few years of intense religious experience, expressed in her lovely poetry. When she discovers that experience might be based upon illness, her world is shaken. Should she have surgery to cure the illness, and risk losing that experience? I enjoyed Salzman's look at the life inside a convent, the way he uses language, and especially his clear-eyed depiction of women who devote their lives to God. It is a book that should cheer anyone who ever has felt discouraged in his or her choice of profession, whatever it is. I may want to revisit Mariette in Ecstasy, by Ron Hanson. </span>

              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer156849495">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15313999075439920622">What happens to your faith and your closeness to God when you find out that this closeness might be a result of a brain tumor? Do you go ahead and have the operation thinking that this rapture might end? These are the questions one finds portrayed in <strong>Lying Awake</strong>.<br /><br />Although quite a short book, this novel packed quite a few things that people have wondered about for ages. Does God exist in our minds and hearts and if so is that enough to get one totally devoted to him? Sister St John of the Cross, i</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText15313999075439920622">What happens to your faith and your closeness to God when you find out that this closeness might be a result of a brain tumor? Do you go ahead and have the operation thinking that this rapture might end? These are the questions one finds portrayed in <strong>Lying Awake</strong>.<br /><br />Although quite a short book, this novel packed quite a few things that people have wondered about for ages. Does God exist in our minds and hearts and if so is that enough to get one totally devoted to him? Sister St John of the Cross, is the main character going through this dilemma. Her faith is the one constant in her life, one that she has worked on for many years. Her attempts to become one with God has been achieved but now she finds that the goal she has reached might be on that was caused by illness, not by anything she has done to be one with God. <br /><br />This is a book that will have you thinking long after you finish the last page. It is ever so hard to give up something you have worked a lifetime for, so what choice would you make? Sister John is faced with this ultimate choice, go on being in God's presence physically, mentally, and emotionally or run the risk that the removal of the tumor will take this heavenly bliss away. <br /><br /></span>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <a href="/user/show/1266077-stephen-gallup" class="user" title="Stephen Gallup">Stephen Gallup</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer139484992">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16991442853903437396">This was a good one to read on the heels of <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/9583486-the-power-and-the-glory"> <em>The Power and The Glory</em> </a>, which I finished a couple weeks ago. Both novels concern hypervigilant, self-critical souls questing for God and unable to take comfort in potential signs of having made progress.<br /><br />Salzman's character is a cloistered nun who for almost 30 years has devoted herself to chanting liturgies, meditating, and writing poetry about the soul's aspiration. She has now begun having severe headaches, which she almost welcomes as a small por</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText16991442853903437396">This was a good one to read on the heels of <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/9583486-the-power-and-the-glory">
  <em>The Power and The Glory</em>
</a>, which I finished a couple weeks ago. Both novels concern hypervigilant, self-critical souls questing for God and unable to take comfort in potential signs of having made progress.<br /><br />Salzman's character is a cloistered nun who for almost 30 years has devoted herself to chanting liturgies, meditating, and writing poetry about the soul's aspiration. She has now begun having severe headaches, which she almost welcomes as a small portion of the cross she seeks to bear. But with them, increasingly, come flashes of vision that could be glimpses into the hereafter. If so, this could be the answer to her lifetime of self-sacrifice.<br /><br />There's a medical explanation, too, however, and the neurologist has a remedy. Of more concern to her is the implication of allowing the visions to continue: It can't be right for her to allow other nuns to become jealous of her gift, or to disrupt the ordered flow of activities in the convent when the condition overtakes her. (Any parallel between her and their patron saint, who may have had the same condition, is of course unthinkable and never mentioned.) On the other hand, she dreads giving them up and resuming her former unanswered quest. Still, taking any pleasure in what could be evidence of God's favor would, by definition, make her undeserving of that favor.<br /><br />Mark Salzman is one of the two living authors I would most like to know in person (the other being Amy Tan). I first encountered him via his delightful coming-of-age memoir <em>Lost in Place</em>, then <em>Iron &amp; Silk</em>, and most recently <em>The Soloist</em>, which is most like the present one. (I see that I gave five stars to <em>The Laughing Sutra</em>, but my memory of that is not as vivid.) In each, I admired the insight he displays, particularly because each intersects with a portion of my own life and relates it in a way I could not.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="16991442853903437396">...more</a>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_93948925">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/93948925" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/3419322-asya" class="left imgcol" title="Asya"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1443579145p2/3419322.jpg" alt="Asya" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/93948925?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Mar 07, 2011</a>

      <a href="/user/show/3419322-asya" class="user" title="Asya">Asya</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/136228.Lying_Awake" title="Paperback 9780747561408" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer93948925">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12889200800363312809">A gorgeous prose poem, a companion piece to the devotional/passionate/erotic poetry of the Beguines, St. John of the Cross, the testaments of all the mystics, medieval to modern, who have balanced sense and ecstasy and tried to make it a narrative and a life, put it into words, make it a daily experience you could live with. I found Sister John believable, human, and hard to pinpoint - traumatized girl, fat girl, mystic, middle aged woman, epilepsy victim, aging nun. For me, Salzman's narrative</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText12889200800363312809">A gorgeous prose poem, a companion piece to the devotional/passionate/erotic poetry of the Beguines, St. John of the Cross, the testaments of all the mystics, medieval to modern, who have balanced sense and ecstasy and tried to make it a narrative and a life, put it into words, make it a daily experience you could live with. I found Sister John believable, human, and hard to pinpoint - traumatized girl, fat girl, mystic, middle aged woman, epilepsy victim, aging nun. For me, Salzman's narrative had more character than the characters, and the passages of poetry, either from the Rule or scripture or Sister John's poetry, was the heart of this novella. Ultimately it was a "slight" novel - revolving around a single decision, a short period of time, a limited cast of characters - but ultimately too I only wanted this narrative to be a prose poem, nothing more. It's not a novel on the scale of Anna Karenina and should not be held to the same standards. Another favorite of the same novella/prose poem genre that comes to mind is Delillo's The Body Artist or some of Jeanette Winterson's work. Too bad we've lost that genre for sales purposes at least.<br /><br />I read this mostly on the subway and at home on a rainy Sunday. Which only reinforced the idea of a prosaic world, grace, epiphany, transformation out of reach - and the book points to how to live with that absence.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="12889200800363312809">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_29168753">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/29168753" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/1037334-k" class="left imgcol" title="K"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1217357273p2/1037334.jpg" alt="K" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/29168753?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Aug 29, 2008</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1037334-k" class="user" title="K">K</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/781593.Lying_Awake" title="Paperback 9780375706066" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer29168753">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18407743352974437454">This is a small, simply written book about a current-day Carmelite nun who has passed years in her monastery feeling disconnected from her faith. She begins to have intense spiritual visions and experiences which she believes is the connection to God she has been working towards her whole life. At the same time these hyper-religious experiences are accompanied by severely debilitating pain, headaches and blackouts. She is forced to seek medical treatment and it is revealed that the visions are c</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText18407743352974437454">This is a small, simply written book about a current-day Carmelite nun who has passed years in her monastery feeling disconnected from her faith. She begins to have intense spiritual visions and experiences which she believes is the connection to God she has been working towards her whole life. At the same time these hyper-religious experiences are accompanied by severely debilitating pain, headaches and blackouts. She is forced to seek medical treatment and it is revealed that the visions are caused by a form of epilepsy which causes religious obsession, visions, hypergraphia. The story hinges around her struggle to reconcile the fact that her euphoric connection to God and faith may simply be an abnormality in her brain.<br /><br />I found that while reading this book, I actually thought about it more when I put it down. When trying to rate this book I hover between the "it was ok"-2 stars and the "liked it"-3 stars; the written story was interesting, but I was actually more interested in the ideas the book provoked - faith, reason, the need for a religious connection, the sometimes pride of religous devotion when your motives should be selfless.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="18407743352974437454">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_87723609">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/87723609" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/13779-k" class="left imgcol" title="K"><img src="http://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_50x66-6a03a5c12233c941481992b82eea8d23.png" alt="K" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/87723609?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Feb 02, 2010</a>

      <a href="/user/show/13779-k" class="user" title="K">K</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/781593.Lying_Awake" title="Paperback 9780375706066" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">people who are interested in questions of spirituality</span></div>

        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/13779-k?shelf=thought-provoking" class="actionLinkLite">thought-provoking</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/13779-k?shelf=spiritualityreligion" class="actionLinkLite">spiritualityreligion</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer87723609">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2212833396588285439">Sister John is a nun who, having struggled with doubt and disillusionment for years, is finally reaching the spiritual heights she envisioned when she first joined the convent. She is an inspiration to those around her, writing prolific devotional poetry to critical acclaim. When a neurological explanation for her newfound spirituality comes to light, everything is suddenly cast into doubt and she struggles with the dilemma of whether to relinquish her gift and have the brain surgery her doctor</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText2212833396588285439">Sister John is a nun who, having struggled with doubt and disillusionment for years, is finally reaching the spiritual heights she envisioned when she first joined the convent. She is an inspiration to those around her, writing prolific devotional poetry to critical acclaim. When a neurological explanation for her newfound spirituality comes to light, everything is suddenly cast into doubt and she struggles with the dilemma of whether to relinquish her gift and have the brain surgery her doctor deems necessary.<br /><br />"Sister John thought: I can't bear the thought of going back to who I was before. I prayed and scrubbed and went through the motions with no feeling of love, only a will to keep busy. If the surgery were to take my dream away, everything I've gone through up to now would seem meaningless...But what is my dream? Is it really to know God, or is it to know personal happiness?" (p. 138)<br /><br />In this short and deceptively accessible novel, Mark Salzman plays with this question of spirituality vs. selfishness from a variety of perspectives. <br /><br />"'...we're all better off having doubts about the state of our souls than presuming ourselves to be holy,'" Sister John's priest tells her when she confides her diagnosis and dilemma to him. "'You allowed yourself to think that loving God meant enjoying His company, having ecstasies. It was all about <em>you,</em> wasn't it? But loving God is supposed to be all about <em>Him.</em>'"<br /><br />Was Sister John selfish to crave spiritually intense experiences as opposed to making her peace with the often mundane nature of subservience to God? This is a question with a great deal of meaning for anyone who strives to be religious or spiritual and live their life for a higher purpose. <br /><br />Salzman looks at this question from other perspectives as well. One nun confesses to feeling inferior to her peers because she cannot tolerate the cloistered lifestyle and prefers to serve the community. A novice nun struggles with her family's accusation that she has retreated to the convent out of a selfish desire for attention and respect, an accusation that stings because it may contain some truth. And Sister John herself, earlier in the story, is confronted by the mother who abandoned her and sorely tempted, legitimately, to tell her off in spite of her religious training. <br /><br />Is it selfish for religious people to want their lifestyle to provide them with personal satisfaction? Or is it simply a natural outgrowth of following a lifestyle in which material sacrifices are made, ostensibly, for the greater good?<br /><br />I feel these are profound questions, and the book's short length and accessible style enhance its power. I did take off a star because I felt somehow distanced from the narrative and the characters where I would have preferred to feel more fully involved. Although I'd like to think that was a deliberate attempt to provide me with a reading experience that parallels that of a spiritual seeker who seeks a more connected experience, I suspect that's a bit of a stretch and would have preferred a more emotionally engaging experience. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="2212833396588285439">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_59193069">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/59193069" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/260625-rachel-wagner" class="left imgcol" title="Rachel Wagner"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1187197063p2/260625.jpg" alt="Rachel Wagner" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/59193069?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jun 10, 2009</a>

      <a href="/user/show/260625-rachel-wagner" class="user" title="Rachel Wagner">Rachel Wagner</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/136228.Lying_Awake" title="Paperback 9780747561408" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer59193069">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4940110973626393209">One of the best books I have read in a long time. I LOVED IT. The story revolves around a nun who comes from a troubled home. As a child she clings to the nuns at school and decides to become one. (Btw this is not the cruel or goofy depiction of nuns we often see. They are good, holy women). As an adult she begins having violent visions of God where she feels compelled to write about what she has seen. To help others she publishes her writings, and they become popular. Unfortunately she finds ou</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText4940110973626393209">One of the best books I have read in a long time. I LOVED IT. The story revolves around a nun who comes from a troubled home. As a child she clings to the nuns at school and decides to become one. (Btw this is not the cruel or goofy depiction of nuns we often see. They are good, holy women). As an adult she begins having violent visions of God where she feels compelled to write about what she has seen. To help others she publishes her writings, and they become popular. Unfortunately she finds out her visions are medical episodes triggered by epilepsy not religious events. This causes a trial in her faith- should she get the surgery and remove her closeness to God, or should she keep the visions but endanger her own health and continually scare her fellow sisters. The lessons she learns about God, visions, spirituality, life, forgiveness and self-acceptance are powerful. She also learns that the impact of her life goes far beyond her writings but that her character has made the world a better place. This is not a syrupy book. It is subtle and mostly contained in the mind of the lead character. I LOVED it! It reminded me a lot of Gilead, Death Comes for the Archbishop and I Heard the Owl Call My Name. I can't figure out why it is out of print. What a shame! Find it and read it! </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="4940110973626393209">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer95850491">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13545263452193105046">Have you ever <em>longed</em> to know God? I mean really <strong>know</strong> Him, feel close to Him, sense His presence within or near you? <br /><br />Sister John of the Cross, a cloistered Carmelite of middle age, waited for many dry years to know her Bridegroom. Then a few years ago she started having migraine-like headaches. With the onset of the headaches, she became able to engage in deep meditation and began having Divine encounters, which led to a best-selling book and notoriety for her convent just outside Los Angeles. <br /><br />Ho</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText13545263452193105046">Have you ever <em>longed</em> to know God? I mean really <strong>know</strong> Him, feel close to Him, sense His presence within or near you? <br /><br />Sister John of the Cross, a cloistered Carmelite of middle age, waited for many dry years to know her Bridegroom. Then a few years ago she started having migraine-like headaches. With the onset of the headaches, she became able to engage in deep meditation and began having Divine encounters, which led to a best-selling book and notoriety for her convent just outside Los Angeles. <br /><br />However, as the severity of head pain continued to increase, Sister John was forced to seek medical attention which led to a disappointing medical diagnosis and an especially difficult decision for a theophilus. <br /><br /><em>Lying Awake</em> is a gentle, yet powerful story, described by Beverly Mantyh in her review <a rel="nofollow" href="http://us.mc583.mail.yahoo.com/mc/welcome?.gx=1&amp;.tm=1270864406&amp;.rand=1aurct2aha78k#_pg=showMessage&amp;sMid=0&amp;fid=ST%2520SMMP%2520EWTN%2520%252b&amp;filterBy=&amp;.rand=94268841&amp;midIndex=0&amp;mid=1_176_AJzsjkQAADEYS6yBvgMSxUnH42Q&amp;f=1&amp;m=1_176_AJzsjkQAADEYS6yBvgMSxUnH42Q,1_1475_AJ3sjkQAAV5PSsDPXAA0M08J7bE,1_2885_AJ3sjkQAAWufSokY7AJZQikPEKQ,1_4248_AJrsjkQAAC3pSoAUfgM%2FgkMucdc,1_5513_AJ7sjkQAADy5Sm%2F%2FiQdjHVytBFc,1_6860_AJzsjkQAAFM5SlsjQQnjdWXHWwA,&amp;sort=date&amp;order=down&amp;startMid=0&amp;hash=f80045a0fd7e3e2b8c515567997d3fdf&amp;.jsrand=4870432">here</a> as more poem than prose. The book is organized around the liturgical calendar and rich in Catholic symbolism, which is why—even though it <em>can</em> be read quickly—it is best appreciated at leisure.<br /><br />Savor it. Better yet, take any one of Sister John’s thoughts—they are all in italics—as a rich source for your daily mediation. Excellent!<br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="13545263452193105046">...more</a>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_1041661352">

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1041661352?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Sep 01, 2014</a>

      <a href="/user/show/559251-terry" class="user" title="Terry">Terry</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/559251-terry?shelf=fiction" class="actionLinkLite">fiction</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1041661352">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17826405950167396092">A spare, even austere, interior novel about a Discalced Camelite nun with an affinity for mystical experiences.- not quite the book I would have expected to be on my list. A trusted friend put a copy in my hands, so I read it. The writing is excellent, spare yet evocative. The setting and content are largely alien to me, but I enjoyed the book without understanding it emotionally.</span>

          </span>

  <span style="display:none" id="freeText100204604655808545">"An absentee father who demands that his children put him at the center of their lives and beg for his return."<br /><br />this quote sums up how i would feel about being a contemplative nun like sister john. although i wouldn't choose such a lifestyle, Salzman treats this topic with great care and complexity. what i enjoyed about the book are the questions it raised about religious life:<br /><br />is it selfish to give up your outside life for one of religious devotion?<br />is a contemplative nun just as worthy as a nun who does deeds within a community? <br />are all religious experiences simply physiological?<br />would i be willing to give up a "gift" that made me special?<br />is suffering equivalent to showing faith?<br /><br />this is the only time that EVERY person in my book club enjoyed a book. that must say something for this unique book. <br /><br /></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer872691898">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13732124021034253864">The story of Sister John, who joins the sisterhood of Carmel to know God more deeply. After experiencing a barren period in her spirituality, she begins to have mystical seizures which she believes to be answered prayer and evidence that she is with God after all. However, due to the extreme nature of the seizures and the following migraines, the mother superior insists that she consult a medical doctor, who diagnoses her with epilepsy and suggests a surgical cure. Sister John must now contempla</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText13732124021034253864">The story of Sister John, who joins the sisterhood of Carmel to know God more deeply. After experiencing a barren period in her spirituality, she begins to have mystical seizures which she believes to be answered prayer and evidence that she is with God after all. However, due to the extreme nature of the seizures and the following migraines, the mother superior insists that she consult a medical doctor, who diagnoses her with epilepsy and suggests a surgical cure. Sister John must now contemplate her decision of whether to have the surgery or forego the procedure in order to keep experiencing the ecstatic state of the fits. Overall, this book gives example of how it is possible to feel as if you are without God, as His mystery deepens.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer1008647521240224926">Sr. John is a cloistered nun who, upon entering the life of a Carmelite, is not finding the spiritual growth and maturity she had expected. She comes from a dysfunctional family, who were probably a strong influence in her choice to become a religious. Years later, she begins to experience visions, powerful meditative states in which she has felt a divine presence, periods of intense creativity and writing, all of which eventually are diagnosed as a form of epilepsy related to a small tumor. Is</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText1008647521240224926">Sr. John is a cloistered nun who, upon entering the life of a Carmelite, is not finding the spiritual growth and maturity she had expected. She comes from a dysfunctional family, who were probably a strong influence in her choice to become a religious. Years later, she begins to experience visions, powerful meditative states in which she has felt a divine presence, periods of intense creativity and writing, all of which eventually are diagnosed as a form of epilepsy related to a small tumor. Is the surgery going to destroy her spiritual gifts? While fictional, Salzman's poetic little book circles around questions of faith and discernment. Beautifully written. </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer4000887397612825362">I am awed by this book and all I could think when I finished it was "perfect". He doesn't need to tell you about the stark precision of the monastery because it's in the style of the prose. He doesn't need to describe the poetry that comes, unbidden, to the protagonist because you experience it at the same time that she does. <br /><br />It's a simple plot line with a deep underlying question that could have easily been elaborated on or agonized over, but that is the key to the book, it's left to you to do</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText4000887397612825362">I am awed by this book and all I could think when I finished it was "perfect". He doesn't need to tell you about the stark precision of the monastery because it's in the style of the prose. He doesn't need to describe the poetry that comes, unbidden, to the protagonist because you experience it at the same time that she does. <br /><br />It's a simple plot line with a deep underlying question that could have easily been elaborated on or agonized over, but that is the key to the book, it's left to you to do all of that. There is not one spare word and I applaud Mark Salzman for his eloquence and the extreme thought and discipline that must have gone into this book. </span>

              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer672628609">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7746898061372414839">A quiet, gently sparkling book; the cloister of Sister John and the internal nature of the narrative (this nun struggling with the realization that her "kaleidoscope" of spiritual visions come from epilepsy) make me feel somewhat claustrophobic, but also make moments of scene and exchange and breaking-in from the outer world wonderfully startling, as if her way of seeing affects your own and you find yourself listening and watching much more intently. The italicized sections from her life before</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText7746898061372414839">A quiet, gently sparkling book; the cloister of Sister John and the internal nature of the narrative (this nun struggling with the realization that her "kaleidoscope" of spiritual visions come from epilepsy) make me feel somewhat claustrophobic, but also make moments of scene and exchange and breaking-in from the outer world wonderfully startling, as if her way of seeing affects your own and you find yourself listening and watching much more intently. The italicized sections from her life before the convent feel contrived at times, and I don't always experience the muted emotional movements, but overall the book is beautiful in its spareness.</span>

This book both inspired and challenged me. I just finished reading it and can't wait to read it again because I'm sure ther</span>

It is short, but the characters are fully developed. You will learn about the daily life of a contemporary nun living in a cloister outside of L.A., as well as how the main character, Sister John, struggles with her doubts and faith. This book both inspired and challenged me. I just finished reading it and can't wait to read it again because I'm sure there is a lot I probably missed.</span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_1488767438">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1488767438" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/4424141-michael" class="left imgcol" title="Michael"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1296524957p2/4424141.jpg" alt="Michael" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1488767438?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jan 01, 2016</a>

      <a href="/user/show/4424141-michael" class="user" title="Michael">Michael</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/136228.Lying_Awake" title="Paperback 9780747561408" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1488767438">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6953470382640945343">I would not have chosen to buy this book. I am not even sure why I picked it up as a Staff Copy at Borders fifteen odd years ago. But there it sat on my shelf, a curiosity in my library that I really never thought about reading. Lying Awake is a book of religious fiction, and until I decided I needed to pick up a novel a few days ago, I had never really considered reading it. I do not read a lot of fiction anymore, and what fiction I do read mostly consists of works I have already read several t</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText6953470382640945343">I would not have chosen to buy this book. I am not even sure why I picked it up as a Staff Copy at Borders fifteen odd years ago. But there it sat on my shelf, a curiosity in my library that I really never thought about reading. Lying Awake is a book of religious fiction, and until I decided I needed to pick up a novel a few days ago, I had never really considered reading it. I do not read a lot of fiction anymore, and what fiction I do read mostly consists of works I have already read several times over: Catch 22, Watership Down, some Steinbeck or Ray Bradbury. I am not a fan of religious fiction at all. Most of the genre is pretty dopey, either apocalyptic mumbo jumbo like the Left Behind series or pious bull that simply retells easily digestible tales of conversion and peace in God. So a book about a Carmelite nun and her struggle to understand God is not really up my alley. In spite of all of this, I thoroughly enjoyed reading Lying Awake.<br /><br />The book is a kind of reverse spiritual struggle, that in the end reminded me of Buddhism. Without giving too much away, the story unfolds when Sister John of the Cross discovers that her religious experiences have a more mundane explanation, one that puts her faith and her way of life in question. The story succeeds, I think, because Salzman captures the humanity of a monastic lifestyle, one which rang true for me as I recalled the stories of the monks of St. John’s Abbey that were told to me as I attended college at St. John’s. I certainly cannot claim to understand if the portrait he paints of the inner motivations of religious people is accurate, but the day to day of living a life dedicated to prayer and obedience felt authentic. And as the book closed, the contemplative life so desired and sought by Sister John takes on a sort of life of action and work that her spiritual gifts had stymied. <br /><br />The book is a fast read. The prose, simple and elegant; Salzman does a nice job of telling a simple story with self-sustaining tempo, that is utterly respectful of the way of life he portrays while ensuring to preserve the humanity of the nuns. I do not think this is going to be the most memorable book I have ever read. I almost certainly will never read it again, but it was a pleasure to read nonetheless. <br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="6953470382640945343">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

This exemplary devotional work summons readers to holiness, reveals ways to conquer self-love and pride, and points the way for experiencing each moment as a sacrament.\n\nThe author shows how God speaks to us through every moment of every day. He encourages a joyous, affirming, selfless abandonment to God. By entering into such active contemplation and facing ourselves honestly and openly, the author assures us, we can achieve the comfort and fulfillment of a life suffused with grace.\n\nThese timeless spiritual reflections probe the mystery of faith, illuminate the nature of authentic contemplation, and help us recognize and carry out God\'s purpose in our own lives. In the prayer lives of many Christians there is one person of the Trinity that often goes overlooked – The Holy Spirit. The Holy Spirit is no less involved in our lives than the Father and the Son, yet many are unaware of this. It will lead you to be more attentive to the movements of the Holy Spirit in your life as you learn to value the third person of the Trinity as the Sanctifier of your soul. Philippe explains the rewards of being attentive to the Holy Spirit and provides simple and concrete ways to grow in this inner sensitivity.     <div class=" clearFloats bigBox" id="aboutAuthor"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/13561.Mark_Salzman">About Mark Salzman</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
      <a href="/author/show/13561.Mark_Salzman"><img alt="13561" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/authors/1269973794p2/13561.jpg" class="right" /></a>
    <div class="readable">
      
<span id="freeTextContainer13269242787815564515">Mark Salzman is an award-winning novelist and nonfiction author who has written on a variety of subjects, from a graceful novel about a Carmelite nun’s ecstatic visions and crisis of faith to a compelling memoir about growing up a misfit in a Connecticut suburb – clearly displaying a range that transcends genre. As a boy, all Salzman ever wanted was to be a Kung Fu master, but it was his proficien</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText13269242787815564515">Mark Salzman is an award-winning novelist and nonfiction author who has written on a variety of subjects, from a graceful novel about a Carmelite nun’s ecstatic visions and crisis of faith to a compelling memoir about growing up a misfit in a Connecticut suburb – clearly displaying a range that transcends genre. As a boy, all Salzman ever wanted was to be a Kung Fu master, but it was his proficiency on the cello that facilitated his acceptance to Yale at the age of 16. He soon changed his major to Chinese language and philosophy, which took him to mainland China where he taught English for two years and studied martial arts. He never gave up music, though, and Salzman’s cello playing appears on the soundtrack to several films, including the Academy Award-winning documentary Breathing Lessons: The Life and Work of Mark O’Brien. He has also played with Yo-Yo Ma and pianist Emanuel Ax at Lincoln Center. Salzman’s unusual combination of talents – as both a well-known author and a concert-proficient cellist – led to a feature profile about him in The New Yorker magazine. He was also recently presented with the Algonquin West Hollywood Literary Award.<br /><br />A number of Mark Salzman’s books have been chosen for “book in common” reading programs by more than a few schools and universities for their elegance, humor, and portrayal of our shared humanity. His first memoir, Iron and Silk, inspired by his years in China, was a finalist for the Pulitzer Prize in nonfiction and received the Christopher Award. His book True Notebooks is a fascinating look at his experiences as a writing teacher at Los Angeles Central Juvenile Hall, a lockup for violent teenage offenders. Salzman is also the author of the memoir Lost in Place: Growing Up Absurd in Suburbia, and the novels The Laughing Sutra, The Soloist, and Lying Awake. Common to each of his works is a theme of how people struggle to reach an ideal but often fall short, and the quiet change that takes place in facing the discouragement and the possibility of never achieving their goal. Salzman writes with gut-wrenching honesty and unalloyed warmth, combined with a sharp sense of humor.<br /><br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="13269242787815564515">...more</a>

    </div>

One of us, Helen, thought is flying blind.”</span>
